If a function, must either\n   328→            work when passed a Series or when passed to Series.apply.\n   329→\n   330→            Accepted combinations are:\n   331→\n   332→            - function\n   333→            - string function name\n   334→            - list of functions and/or function names, e.g. ``[np.sum, 'mean']``\n   335→            - None, in which case ``**kwargs`` are used with Named Aggregation. Here\n   336→              the output has one column for each element in ``**kwargs``. The name of\n   337→              the column is keyword, whereas the value determines the aggregation\n   338→              used to compute the values in the column.\n   339→\n   340→              Can also accept a Numba JIT function with\n   341→              ``engine='numba'`` specified. Only passing a single function is supported\n   342→              with this engine.\n   343→\n   344→              If the ``'numba'`` engine is chosen, the function must be\n   345→              a user defined function with ``values`` and ``index`` as the\n   346→              first and second arguments respectively in the function signature.\n   347→              Each group's index will be passed to the user defined function\n   348→              and optionally available for use.\n   349→\n   350→        *args\n   351→            Positional arguments to pass to func.\n   352→        engine : str, default None\n   353→            * ``'cython'`` : Runs the function through C-extensions from cython.\n   354→            * ``'numba'`` : Runs the function through JIT compiled code from numba.\n   355→            * ``None`` : Defaults to ``'cython'`` or globally setting\n   356→                ``compute.use_numba``\n   357→\n   358→        engine_kwargs : dict, default None\n   359→            * For ``'cython'`` engine, there are no accepted ``engine_kwargs``\n   360→            * For ``'numba'`` engine, the engine can accept ``nopython``, ``nogil``\n   361→              and ``parallel`` dictionary keys. The values must either be ``True`` or\n   362→              ``False``. The default ``engine_kwargs`` for the ``'numba'`` engine is\n   363→              ``{'nopython': True, 'nogil': False, 'parallel': False}`` and will be\n   364→              applied to the function\n   365→\n   366→        **kwargs\n   367→            * If ``func`` is None, ``**kwargs`` are used to define the output names and\n   368→              aggregations via Named Aggregation. See :ref:`gotchas.udf-mutation`\n   396→        for more details.\n   397→\n   398→        The resulting dtype will reflect the return value of the passed ``func``,\n   399→        see the examples below.\n   400→\n   401→        Examples\n   402→        --------\n   403→        >>> s = pd.Series([1, 2, 3, 4])\n   404→\n   405→        >>> s\n   406→        0    1\n   407→        1    2\n   408→        2    3\n   409→        3    4\n   410→        dtype: int64\n   411→\n   412→        >>> s.groupby([1, 1, 2, 2]).min()\n   413→        1    1\n   414→        2    3\n   415→        dtype: int64\n   416→\n   417→        >>> s.groupby([1, 1, 2, 2]).agg(\"min\")\n   418→        1    1\n   419→        2    3\n   420→        dtype: int64\n   421→\n   422→        >>> s.groupby([1, 1, 2, 2]).agg([\"min\", \"max\"])\n   423→           min  max\n   424→        1    1    2\n   425→        2    3    4\n   426→\n   427→        The output column names can be controlled by passing\n   428→        the desired column names and aggregations as keyword arguments.\n   429→\n   430→        >>> s.groupby([1, 1, 2, 2]).agg(\n   431→        ...     minimum=\"min\",\n   432→        ...     maximum=\"max\",\n   433→        ... )\n   434→           minimum  maximum\n   435→        1        1        2\n   436→        2        3        4\n   437→\n   438→        The resulting dtype will reflect the return value of the aggregating\n   439→        function.\n   440→\n   441→        >>> s.groupby([1, 1, 2, 2]).agg(lambda x: x.astype(float).min())\n   442→        1    1.0\n   443→        2    3.0\n   444→        dtype: float64\n   445→        \"\"\"\n   446→        relabeling = func is None\n   447→        columns = None\n   448→        if relabeling:\n   449→            columns, func = validate_func_kwargs(kwargs)\n   450→            kwargs = {}\n   451→\n   452→        if isinstance(func, str):\n   453→            if maybe_use_numba(engine) and engine is not None:\n   454→                # However, we could potentially recast PyObject* as size_t* and back.\n\nLooking at the parsers example more carefully, they use kh_strbox_t which has:\n```c\nctypedef struct kh_strbox_t:\n    khuint_t n_buckets, size, n_occupied, upper_bound\n    uint32_t *flags\n    kh_cstr_t *keys\n    PyObject **vals  # This stores Python objects!\n```\n\nSo kh_strbox_t is designed to store Python objects as values. But it expects string keys.\n\nFor our case with arbitrary Python object keys and numpy array values, we have a few options:\n\n1. Use kh_pymap_t and create a wrapper class to hold the arrays, then store references to those\n\n\n2. Keep the dictionary but optimize the iteration logic\n3. Create a custom optimization that builds indices more efficiently\n\nRe-reading the reference about \"Replaced Python dictionary-based aggregation with Cython-optimized hash table lookup using pandas' internal khash implementation\" - this suggests the optimization focuses on using khash for lookups rather than replacing dictionary storage entirely. In `indices_fast()`, we're taking labels and keys, building groups based on label changes, and for each group creating a key (tuple or scalar).\n\nThe performance issues likely stem from tuple creation overhead and repeated dictionary operations. A more efficient approach would leverage khash to track unique group combinations upfront, reducing tuple allocations and dictionary lookups.
